B005FITILO|5.0|Mavis Gary is the ghostwriter for a fading series of Y.A. novels.  Aware of the depression she is sinking into (but not exactly of how far into it she's already gone), Mavis thinks of the happiest time of her life: when she was in high school, dating the popular boy, Buddy Slade.  Despite the fact that Buddy has a wife and new child, Mavis heads back to the small town of Mercury to reclaim the lost days of her youth.<br /><br />It's a basic, even boring premise, yet Jason Reitman's YOUNG ADULT captures the complexities of depression/self-discovery perfectly, ripe with all the awkward moments, self-loathing, and even humor that goes along with it.  To call YOUNG ADULT a comedy would be doing it a disservice, as it is far more than that: it's an intricate, layered character study, anchored by Charlize Theron's lead performance.  That Theron wasn't even nominated for a Best Actress Oscar is almost a tragedy; the same can be said for Patton Oswalt, who goes from stand-up comedic to dramatic actor as the flip-side of Mavis: a kid who was largely ignored, even picked on, with tragic results.  Theron and Oswalt give deep, mesmerizing performances; also strong are Patrick Wilson (a grossly-underused actor) as Buddy, and Elizabeth Reaser as his wife.  Reitman, as usual, handles his actors perfectly, working from another solid script by Diablo Cody.  This is the team that brought you JUNO, yes, but this is definitely a film for adults--for only adults can know the pain of discovering that who you see when you look in the mirror is only a glimmer of who you are.  Uncomfortable at times, hilarious at others, YOUNG ADULT is not a film to be missed, under any circumstances.

0142000655|5.0|Most people will never read \\"East of Eden\\". If they want to find out why John Steinbeck is celebrated as an American icon of the literary world they'll usually pick up something a little less daunting like \\"The Pearl\\", \\"Tortilla Flat\\" or one of his other smaller books. Even if they are willing to spend time on one of his more epic works, they usually turn to \\"The Grapes of Wrath\\". That is a shame, not because his other works are great; they certainly are that, but because \\"East of Eden\\" is a masterpiece that tackles the age old issue of the dual nature of man in a manner that we can all related to.<br />Where books like \\"The Brothers Karamazov\\" and \\"Crime and Punishment\\", tackling the same issue, are weighty and hard to navigate (Don't get me wrong, they are worth the effort), \\"East of Eden\\" is engrossing and captivating. Using the story of Cain and Abel in Genesis not only as part of the story but as the basis for his characters Aaron and Caleb (Their father's name is Adam), Steinbeck explores the balance between wanting to do good and doing evil as well as the motivations behind them redefining them to make us look at both in a new light.<br />Cain, in the Bible, takes on a new life and I found myself able to understand better why he did what he did and oddly enough, relating to him. While I've never killed my brother (There are certainly times I wanted to!), I can understand the feelings of rejection he had that brought him to that place and, after all, I think that this is part of the reason it's in the Bible, not so that we can set ourselves above him but rather understand that the nature of Cain is in all of us and that God loves us in spite of it (Cain, after all, was protected by God. The \\"mark of Cain\\" wasn't to brand him a killer but rather to mark him as a man protected by God).<br />Steinbeck does, at times, ramble; the book is, granted, a daunting one but it is also a classic for a reason. Someone once said \\"We read to know that we are not alone\\" and Icouldn't agree more. There is a quote that I had stenciled on the wall of my study where my 3,000 volumes (and growing)reside which reads \\"The man who does not read good books has no advantage over the man who can't read them.\\" It is attributed to Mark Twain but it's questionable whether it came from him or not. Either way, it's true. If you are not illiterate (you wouldn't be able to read this if you were) than count yourself one of the blessed and fortunate, then, go one step further and pick up \\"East of Eden\\" and read a book that is not only a \\"good book\\" but a true masterpiece.

B003L16FAE|5.0|(EDIT) 12/19/2013. Now that Predator 3D has been released, I thought a brief update to this review might be useful.<br /><br />I was surprised at the quality of the 3D conversion, given that it used the automated JVC Fox workstation that was used for Jumper 3D and I, Robot 3D. The process costs about a third of what a typical catalog 3D conversion costs and can be done with about 3 people in 3 months. In contrast, Titanic took hundreds of people over a year to convert.<br /><br />The slow rate of adoption of 3D has been attributed, in part, to the lack of available content. Fox Home Entertainment with JVC developed a business model using automated equipment that allowed the 3D conversion of catalog movies profitably.<br />I was doubtful that they would be successful in this after watching 3D clips of I, Robot shortly after it was released. I changed my mind after purchasing and watching Predator 3D.<br /><br />The only caveat is that the extras are provided via a Disk two DVD. (Disk one is the 3D/2D disk which has no extras). It would have been better to include the Ultimate Hunter Edition Blu-ray as Disk two as they did for the European release, since it has several more extras, some of them in High Definition. (END EDIT)<br /><br />So, you have recently upgraded your home theater for blu-ray and want to replace your old Predator DVD. Should you buy the 2008 release or the 2010 (UHE) release? Perhaps I can help you decide.<br />When the 2008 edition was released, blu-ray disks were purchased primarily by film purists who believed nothing in the original film should be altered. Today, blu-ray is mainstream and the film purists represent only 4% of the market . Fox, in 2010 was intent on providing a release, Ultimate Hunter Edition, that catered to the tastes of the majority of consumers. The film purist minority is unhappy with that release as well.<br />I saw the movie in the theater when it was released in 1987. The graininess of the image was disturbing. After some research, I found that the film was shot with a film stock that, in low light conditions created an excessively grainy image and that it was not the filmmaker's intent to do that for artistic reasons.<br />I own both the 2008 and the UHE blu-rays and have compared them exhaustively. In my opinion, The UHE is the superior disk in terms of color accuracy and detail.<br />The last word from Fox on the Predator UHE blu-ray comes from Vincent Marcais, Sr. V.P. for International Sales in a taped interview.  \\"The film stock (source for the transfer) was poor. The criticism is unfair. The filmmaker's were consulted, which we always do while they are alive. The UHE is how the filmmaker's wanted it to look\\"<br />Lets put aside the fact that the UHE has 5 hours of supplemental information and the 2008 version has none; and the fact that the UHE uses a modern video codec (AVC MPEG 4 vs VC-1 for 2008) and has a higher bit rate, resulting in more potential detail.<br />Your choice if you consider yourself a film purist is the 2008 release. If you want to enjoy the movie as the director and cinematographer intended, your choice is the Ultimate Hunter Edition. If you are still conflicted, buy both.<br />In conclusion. I own both, but I only watch the Ultimate Hunter Editon.<br /><br />I would caution you about visiting other forums to help you make a purchasing decision. The people on those forums represent only 4% of those that purchase blu-ray disks today and most are biased in favor of the film purists view.<br /><br />Edit. 5/19/2012. I thought it might be interesting to some of you to read some technical details about the difficulty with the source used for the BD and the issue of director's intent:<br /><br />Perhaps this will put to rest any uncertainty about the director's intent regarding grain/grittiness.<br /><br />\\"As I think much has been said online seemingly `authoritatively' about the Director's intent and the use of the film \\"stock\\" to make Predator intentionally look gritty or grainy. Well, that's pretty much inaccurate speculation according to someone I recently spoke with intimately involved in the production.<br />The DP chose the Eastman Kodak stocks he did for the simple fact of capturing \\"usable\\" images on film under relatively low lighting conditions in the jungle...'grit' or `intentional' graininess was never intended in any artistic sense for `atmosphere'. If anything, it was an undesirable side effect of the acquisition because it caused \\"murkiness\\" esp. in the greys and blacks of dark jungle sequences and didn't allow the filmmakers the opportunity to cut the different stocks together in the same scene, which would have been nice to have had that flexibility. To give you an idea of the challenge, they were apparently restricted to shooting at a max. of only T2 in anything but high noon and the middle of the day.<br /><br />Don McAlpine shot the film with 35mm Kodak 5247 and 5294, depending on the lighting conditions available, with or without the aid of artificial light illumination. The '94 (400T) was used for really low lit conditions (like those night sequences in the ravine at the end which apparently were only getting 7 or 8 footcandles down there, with only 4 or 5 footcandles after the smoke starting flowing during the action) and the '47 (125T) stock was used for the set-ups involving wide clearances which had more natural light and as well, could be more easily illuminated with artificial light, when justified. You see, once you add artificial light in the deep jungle, all it accomplishes is lighting the foreground to such an excessive degree that it looks obvious and fake to audiences. Lighting considerations were where the real photographic `artistry\\", if you will, of the captured imagery took place, not in any consideration of grain being used for a `gritty' look.\\"<br /><br />Vincent Marcais Senior V.P. International Marketing, was asked about the policy when remastering catalog titles and specifically about Predator. The Fox executive acknowledges that they got a lot of criticism, \\"but I don't think it was deserved,\\" he countered, because the movie was shot on a film stock that wasn't good enough, and the Blu-ray version \\"represents what the filmmaker wanted it to look like.\\" He said that Fox discusses the issue of \\"removing grain or not\\" with the filmmakers, \\"as long as they are alive\\".<br /><br />Corporate Execs at this level are very careful about the statements they make for publication. There will be no remaster or new transfer of Predator in the foreseeable future based on his comment.

0307387178|5.0|In \\"Into the Wild,\\" author Jon Krakauer has done a masterful job of plumbing the possible personality that might be moved to behaviors that on the surface appear to many of us as acts of suicidal insanity.  He does this by drawing parallels between his own younger days and Chris McCandless's as well as by looking at a few other examples from history.  Writing with a sympathetic view toward his subject that few writers can match, Krakauer manages to shed light on the motives of McCandless without either lionizing or vilifying the man for what he did.<br /><br />I thought Krakauer was very clear in his approach and in what he was trying to do - shed light on what seems a dark mystery to many people.  But a lot of reviewers feel the need to moralize; they cannot resist \\"taking sides\\" for or against  McCandless (or Jon Krakauer, for that matter) in some kind of assumed debate concerning the right or wrong, the sane or crazy, regarding Alex's final adventure.  And this is puzzling to me.  It's hardly a secret that we live in an age of widespread moral relativism.  To some of us it seems that nothing is commonly held as sacred any more.  Certainly the choice to divorce one's spouse is hardly frowned upon by society today.  But if a man can choose to divorce and remarry and even live a double life why is he forgiven while his (much less mature) son cannot be extended similar forgiveness for \\"divorcing\\" his parents because his \\"entire childhood seem[ed] like a fiction\\" due to the marital deception practiced by his parents?  The quotes are Chris McCandless's own words to his sister.  It seems to me we should try to forgive in both cases, but apparently many readers aren't willing to do that or likely don't even see the inequity here.  Or maybe some feel a need to slap a psychological diagnosis on McCandless or to otherwise conveniently categorize or label him lest we find we indeed have much in common with him; the practice of dividing into \\"us\\" and \\"them\\" camps has a certain way of conferring a sense of security.<br /><br />I find it rather easy to sympathize with McCandless without for one moment agreeing with what he did - but I never found reason to blame my parents for what I, too, often saw as a less-than-real world.  It seems to me that McCandless' rejection of society was just an extension of his inability to forgive his father, who, after all, represents the entirety of the world at large to a young impressionable male.  Of course that's throwing the baby out with the bathwater, but young people find it difficult to make such distinctions.  And really, so do a good many older adults - for example, how many of us vote Democrat or Republican not because we \\"believe\\" in the party platform but because we hate more what the Other Party stands for?  Is such an act not at core one of rebellion?<br /><br />Anyway, that's how I read Alex's character.  He refused thing like a decent map, adequate preparation, etc. because that was all part of the \\"fiction\\" of the world that belonged to the \\"fictional\\" existence embodied by his parents.  Maybe the abandoned bus appealed to him because it, like he, was an \\"outcast\\" from that world.  I think he might have felt the need to find just how little he could do with - because then he could be certain that such a basic need was \\"real\\" and not merely part of the \\"fiction\\" against which he was rebelling.  It's sad that he felt this way, if indeed he did, because such a person is just about impossible to reach.  But judging from my recollection of my own mindset of earlier years, I'd say there are many of us that, broadly speaking, either agree or at least sympathize with him now or at some time in our past.<br /><br />Although some reviewers have taken Krakauer to task for the insertion of his own story in the book, it certainly sheds much light on the person of Alex.  If we can wave off Chris McCandless without so much as a second thought, we certainly cannot do the same to Krakauer, if only because he has succeeded in the world in which McCandless/Alex could not get himself to fit.  I find especially illuminating Krakauer's statement that \\"The danger bathed the world in a halogen glow .....  [t]he world was made real\\" regarding his own climbing escapades.  Perhaps McCandless felt like that; one cannot be sure.  But there is surely something about going to extremes that turns one's perceptions up a notch or two.  Or perhaps the immediacy of living of his tramping life let Alex forget what he considered the \\"fiction\\" of his life.  However he saw it, it seems to me that this \\"fiction\\" was in some way in his mind connected to his references of \\"killing the false beast within\\" that he penned while staying on the bus.  And I think it's pretty clear that McCandless thought of his adventures as a way of trying to purge whatever it was that was gnawing at him.  Except for the extreme nature of his adventure, that's hardly foreign to more \\"normal\\" human experiences.  The practice of fasting and other forms of asceticism have a long tradition, and are often rooted in in the context of religious or other spiritual exercises.<br /><br />But the above is my own reading and opinion of the character of Chris/Alex; Krakauer didn't try to force his interpretation on the reader.  Kudos to him for writing in a way that avoids the temptation of excessive diagnostic reporting.  Kudos too for his efforts to make parallels with his own life.  I very much appreciate his references to his own struggles with his father while avoiding baring his soul more than necessary; he refers to \\"long-held family secrets\\" while sparing us the details.  Many another writer could not have resisted telling all; but Krakauer recognized that it was enough to make his point that whatever the secrets were, they affected him deeply and moved him to rage against his father, something that he held in common with Alex, something to which he assumed many of his readers could also relate that might make the main character in some way more accessible.<br /><br />For such a difficult subject matter, I can't see how any author could have done a better job.  Working with only the thinnest amount of source material, Krakauer managed to write a book that seems faithful to its subject.  Several reviewers have complained that the author's use of other examples, including his own, are mere \\"filler\\" to bring the book up to a minimum number of pages, but I don't see it that way at all - the side stories are absolutely essential to shed light on the central subject.  Besides, the narrative portion of the story would have been pretty dull anyway, because the book was hardly about whether Alex/Chris \\"made it\\" or how he otherwise might have, but rather about what internal dynamics drove him.  No matter how much material Chris left, any such an \\"objective\\" rendering of the story, which some seem to want, would never have amounted to shedding any light on the \\"why\\" of it all, which is of course exactly that for which such a story simply begs.<br /><br />I believe I'll give this book five stars, because I sincerely believe it could hardly have been done better - it is as close to perfect as possible.  Thank you, Mr. Krakauer.e he has succeeded in the world in which McCandless/Alex could not get himself to fit.  I find especially illuminating Krakauer's statement that \\"The danger bathed the world in a halogen glow .....  [t]he world was made real\\" regarding his own climbing escapades.  Perhaps McCandless felt like that; one cannot be sure.  But there is surely something about going to extremes that turns one's perceptions up a notch or two.  Or perhaps the immediacy of living of his tramping life let Alex forget what he considered the \\"fiction\\" of his life.  However he saw it, it seems to me that this \\"fiction\\" was in some way in his mind connected to his references of \\"killing the false beast within\\" that he penned while staying on the bus.  And I think it's pretty clear that McCandless thought of his adventures as a way of trying to purge whatever it was that was gnawing at him.  Except for the extreme nature of his adventure, that's hardly foreign to more \\"normal\\" human experiences.  The practice of fasting and other forms of asceticism have a long tradition, and are often rooted in in the context of religious or other spiritual exercises.<br /><br />But the above is my own reading and opinion of the character of Chris/Alex; Krakauer didn't try to force his interpretation on the reader.  Kudos to him for writing in a way that avoids the temptation of excessive diagnostic reporting.  Kudos too for his efforts to make parallels with his own life.  I very much appreciate his references to his own struggles with his father while avoiding baring his soul more than necessary; he refers to \\"long-held family secrets\\" while sparing us the details.  Many another writer could not have resisted telling all; but Krakauer recognized that it was enough to make his point that whatever the secrets were, they affected him deeply and moved him to rage against his father, something that he held in common with Alex, something to which he assumed many of his readers could also relate that might make the main character in some way more accessible.<br /><br />For such a difficult subject matter, I can't see how any author could have done a better job.  Working with only the thinnest amount of source material, Krakauer managed to write a book that seems faithful to its subject.  Several reviewers have complained that the author's use of other examples, including his own, are mere \\"filler\\" to bring the book up to a minimum number of pages, but I don't see it that way at all - the side stories are absolutely essential to shed light on the central subject.  Besides, the narrative portion of the story would have been pretty dull anyway, because the book was hardly about whether Alex/Chris \\"made it\\" or how he otherwise might have, but rather about what internal dynamics drove him.  No matter how much material Chris left, any such an \\"objective\\" rendering of the story, which some seem to want, would never have amounted to shedding any light on the \\"why\\" of it all, which is of course exactly that for which such a story simply begs.<br /><br />I believe I'll give this book five stars, because I sincerely believe it could hardly have been done better - it is as close to perfect as possible.  Thank you, Mr. Krakauer.

B003Y5H5HO|3.0|A Hollywood remake of a Swedish movie made just two years ago, is the first part of the best selling trilogy Millennium, by the late Swedish author (and journalist) Stieg Larsson. The plot is mostly about how investigative journalist Michael Blomkvist (clearly an alter ego of Larsson) is asked by Henrik Vanger, an aging captain of industry to solve the murder of his favorite niece forty years ago. By some strange circumstances, he will be helped in the investigation by the pierced, troubled hacker Lisbeth Salander, with whom professional cooperation will soon turn into the personal as they discover the very dark secrets running under the very successful Vanger family. The Hollywood version is also set in Sweden, though here all the characters speak in English.<br /><br />As much as I like the original movie (I haven't read the books) I must admit that its view of the world is a bit naive, equivalent to an earnest teenager shouting that the whole world is corrupt. In the US version, Blomkvist is played by Daniel Craig (a fine actor who has made a career playing macho guys) as a stronger and more attractive man than in the Swedish movie (where he was played by the older and somewhat chubby Michael Nyqvist). On the other hand, the Lisbeth Salander character is played in the remake by Rooney Mara as a weaker woman than in the original (she might be a fine actress, but she doesn't show it here, despite critical acclaim for her performance). Noomi Rapace made a far stronger impression as Lisbeth in the Swedish movie.<br /><br />Though there are not major changes in the movie plot, the Hollywood version does make small variations, which I think are generally for the worse. For instance, when Craig tries to understand the meaning of certain numbers that might be a clue to the murder, his daughter, who is a fervent catholic going to a religious camp, alerts him that they are a biblical reference. This scene is unlikely, since there are very few Catholics in Sweden, or very religious people for that matter, as it is a very secular country. In the original version, the character of the daughter does not appear, and is Lisbeth, by surfing in the net, that finds the religious connections.<br /><br />The director is David Fincher. He has occasionally made fine movies (for instance, Zodiac) but most of the time his work tend to the flashy and empty (Seven, Fight Club), the overrated (The Social Network) if not to the directly mediocre (Benjamin Button). This movie is not totally worthless, but it is disappointing, and it is considerably weaker than the original version.

That judge trumps up a false charge against the barber and has him locked away so that he can advance upon his wife.  Years later, this barber is back in London seeking revenge under a new name, Sweeney Todd.  With the help of a downtrodden baker (who's pies are the worst around), Todd sets up his shop of massacre, targeting the men responsible for his emotional demise and the apparent suicide of his wife. 0307352145|1.0|I came across the ad for this book while riding the train and it got me intrigued. Somehow, I am not sure the author did full justice to the topic she intended to cover.<br /><br />The start was great when the author lists the `successful' introverts. Her message was to embrace it and make the best of your being an introvert - that you can be successful even if you are not the outgoing kind. I felt it was good to know why we are what we are from a scientific point of view but after a point I found the studies and data pages to be overwhelming. Asian kids are found to be the introverted kind whereas the Americans more outgoing. I am not sure whether I agree with this. I found the author stepping (or confusing herself) from cultural to introvert a little too conveniently. What she deems as being introvert I would label it more of a cultural issue where a people and society \\"consciously\\" place a large amount of emphasis to excel in the field of education whereas in the American/Western approach we are  more holistic stressing on the overall development of the child therefore the exposure to sports, arts and extra-curricular activities in general that can appear extroverted.<br /><br />I also took exception to the Asian and American proverbs used in order to compare introverts and extroverts. The author misses the point that the Asian proverbs have a foundation of deep \\"conscious\\" cultural and philosophical thought behind them. It is a mark of a culture and the \\"choice\\" of a people to the dedication of deep thought and spiritual curiosity not a state of being an introvert. If the author had delved a little more deeply in Asian/Indian studies she would have found that emphasis of education is not restricted to the introverted types but as a foundation for all (community, society) to excel in life. Though the idea of a \\"good education = good job\\" is largely western and the driving force in today's world, for generations Asians and Indians pursued knowledge for a whole lot more than its pragmatic offerings.<br /><br />In continuation of the above thought I wish the author would have researched on the aim and benefits of Yoga and compared them to introverts. It would provide an interesting topic of research.  If I like practicing Yoga, to acquire a state of calm and balance in life, a life totally calm and focused inward, does that make me an introvert? It has been proven that yoga for a large part changes a person's physiological and psychological state from an active person (extrovert) to somebody calmer (introvert?). How does this compare to the physiological state of an introvert?<br /><br />Dedicating a chapter to the Recession of 2008 was a little too tacky for me. To blame extroverted people as a cause for the Great Recession, Enron etc seemed too simplistic. At what point does moral accountability, responsible leadership or a sense of empathy come in the picture? To say that if introverts were in charge of the economy that things would be different is a little stretched for me. One counter point that comes to mind is, who devised the intricate products, ABCP, Risky derivatives? I doubt it was an extrovert.<br /><br />I was not too comfortable on the advice to introverts to pretend to be extroverts because successful introverts have done them. Without weighing in on the moral implications I found it too easy of an advice and generally self-defeating. Somehow it sent the message you really cannot make a living being an introvert.<br /><br />Lastly, I wish the book would have a chapter on the deeper problems that introverts face - depression, doubt etc. The book highlights on mostly the outward aspect - public speaking, being more aggressive in communicating etc but being an introvert, living in one's own world has its own set of demons. How does an introvert fight the self-doubts regardless of outward situations. How does an introvert cope with the struggle to act or not to act? Do introverts get more depressed than extroverts? I wish the author would not have been `Quiet' on these issues?<br /><br />I seriously think the book could be far more deep than in its current version. The inner world is a mystery that only now has seriously begun piquing peoples' interest. A lot needs to be done and explored. Personally, I would have me think as an introvert but there are people who laugh at me when I say that. I justify that comment as I have found myself shut in a room for hours on end working quietly on a project but at the same time I can totally unwind and be part of the sales group at the local bar. I spend time writing, thinking deeply and am naturally attracted to topics considered deep but yet I have little problem connecting with people on mundane issues. I would conclude that one side does not fit all. And to reiterate if the author ever wants to write a sequel I would advise her to study Eastern Philosophy. A lot of insights can be gained on the evolution of human consciousness in its various states. She would see that Introversion = Eastern and Extroversion = Western would have a totally different meaning.

0385523912|5.0|By interviewing North Korean defectors and focusing on a few who have settled in South Korea, Demick has managed to give readers a look into one of the world's most totalitarian and secretive regimes. She does this by retracing the stories of six defectors, reconstructing their journeys through extensive interviews and substantiating their claims with original reporting and research. The result is an engaging, immensely readable work that lays bare the horrors of the North Korean regime.<br /><br />In the end, Demick's work makes you realize that North Koreans, despite having been brainwashed by their government all their lives, are a very intelligent and resilient people. More likely than not, they no longer believe the propaganda forced on them. Those gutsy enough to escape are doing so, and the rest are simply waiting around for the day when the regime collapses -- hoping they'll survive until then.<br /><br />Highly recommended for those curious about the last Communist dictatorship on earth.

0465011047|4.0|Kang Chol-hwan has written a gripping account of his ten years imprisoned in a North Korean labor camp, much of that while he was just a child. The details he provides give a glimpse into both the horrors of the regime and the depths of unimaginable suffering that humans are capable of inflicting upon one another, especially in a system where those being tortured are viewed as little more than cockroaches. What's most shocking is that within 150 years of socialism's conception its principles have been perverted into yet another means of justifying oppression against people who are perceived as being \\"different\\".<br /><br />Although a strong narrative overall, I had some concerns with the book. One is that, as translated from the French, the prose can seem somewhat stilted and awkward. Some vocabulary errors seem to also have escaped the proofreader's eye; for example, in English, taking attendance is \\"roll call\\", not \\"role call\\". \\"Role call\\" would make no sense, because the attendance register is the \\"roll\\". Secondly, because the account is a first-person narrative, told to French journalist Pierre Rigoulot, the book sometimes lacks the detail and rigor that would come from research & reporting. As such, Demick's [[ASIN:0385523912 Nothing to Envy: Ordinary Lives in North Korea]] is a much richer account of life inside North Korea, although, of course, she could have nothing to say about the inner workings of a prison camp. The lack of detail is also hampered by the fact that Kang was very young when first confined to the camp, and who among us remembers specific details about our lives as nine-year-olds?<br /><br />Overall, I'm hesitant to recommend this book, unless you are very interested at the specific conditions inside a North Korean labor camp.

B006ZCWU5K|4.0|There's not much really to not like about \\"Wrecking Ball.\\" Unless it is that this doesn't sound much like a Bruce Springsteen anyone has ever heard before. Or Bruce is getting too political. He tries a lot of new things out. Thank God. Artists have their nuances. If anyone fails to listen to \\"Wrecking Ball\\" out of political mixed feelings or a sense they have philosophical axes to grind with Springsteen, however, they're missing out on a nice bunch of Springsteen songs.<br /><br />The record is more about breaking new ground than it is about taking sides. If he has a comfort zone, Springsteen isn't enjoying it on this album. There has been very little mention of \\"This Depression\\" in most reviews I've read. I think it's because the song is so lacking a familiar Springsteen point of reference. There's nothing Boss-related in \\"This Depression,\\" which features beautiful siren-sounds welling up beneath a confession of despair and plea for help.<br /><br />The first few songs on \\"Wrecking Ball\\"-- \\"We Take Care of Our Own,\\" \\"Easy Money,\\" and \\"Quartered and Drawn\\"-- stick to areas that people relate to when they think about the Boss. There he goes, stirring up depression-era rivalries and partisan working-man politics, defending the downtrodden, going after the privileged. Catching an earful of the political-sounding blast of \\"Wrecking Ball\\"s opening numbers, some listeners may feel it's an angry left-wing protest album, and resolve to pass it by.<br /><br />\\"We Take Care of Our Own,\\" \\"Death to My Hometown,\\" and \\"Jack of All Trades,\\" it is true, appear to march off to battle against the fat cats and Wall Street scoundrels who laid waste to America.  \\"Easy Money\\" ridicules the alluring pipe dreams that came true for a few. But \\"Rocky Ground\\" speaks to everyone in whom something is entrusted, no matter their rung of life, as it cautions, \\"Tend to your flock or they will stray, we'll be called for our service come Judgement Day, Before we cross that river wide, blood on our hands will come back on us twice.\\" Immediately after the word \\"twice\\" a beautiful horn arrangement makes good on the proclamation earlier on in the song of \\"angels singing 'Glory Hallelujah.'\\" The \\"I'm a Soldier\\" refrain in this song gets a little old for my liking, but it's kind of cool the first couple of times. One of the finest works of Springsteen's imagination. Thus far.<br /><br />\\"We Take Care of our Own\\" sounds a little bit ironic, sarcastic even, for Springsteen. Some may wonder, have we come this far only to end up in .  .  . in Randy Newman territory?<br /><br />Maybe not. Who does takes care of their own? Bruce Springsteen and the E. Street Band? Street Gangs? The Cosa Nostra? (It is an Army motto.)<br /><br />\\"Jack of All Trades\\" is a snapshot of a  guy who is out of work and now must turn to the humblest of tasks in order to provide for his family. Luckily he's good at them. It's notable how many words he is given to sing without saying anything. Trying to turn lemons into lemonade he goes on and on, verse after verse, muttering feel-good platitudes and mixed up pieties (Jesus didn't mention \\"might\\"). It's as if our man senses if he ever stopped talking his real feelings might pop out and he and everyone else would know. When finally he does gets around to saying what is really on his mind, he does so in the measured tones of a stoic handy man capable of handling his circumstances. The soaring, weeping guitar that immediately follows his confession conveys that there is a more passionate story to tell.<br /><br />The best numbers on \\"Wrecking Ball\\" for getting up and getting down are, oddly enough, \\"Quartered and Drawn\\" and \\"Death to my Home Town.\\"<br /><br />\\"Land of Hopes and Dreams\\" is a number with a familiar feel to it. The sonic centerpiece and emotional high point of \\"Wrecking Ball,\\" it harks back to another time and place. A time when Clarence Clemons was alive. (And so was his home town.)<br /><br />A few days ago, I was looking at a video of \\"Land of Hopes and Dreams.\\" It was from a Springsteen/E. Street Band concert about a decade ago. It was a far different song from the version on \\"Wrecking Ball.\\" The blissed-out E-Streeter strummers behind Bruce were jumping around, banging away happily at the silly-sounding guitar-trills that dressed up the song. More innocent times .  .  . That's gone here, in favor of a big time, pull out all the stops production. It's assured that Clarence Clemons gets a fitting spotlight on the record.<br /><br />The closing song, \\"We Are Alive,\\" is on its own musically (while lyrically it stakes out a spot a bit to the other end of the universe from Bankers' Hill).<br /><br />At first hearing, I thought that \\"We are Alive\\" was a bit too lightweight & goofy, especially to be the final song. Is \\"Wrecking Ball\\" going to go out with a whimper and not a bang?  On second thought, my memory drifted to the song \\"Good Night\\"  at the end of the Beatles' \\"White Album.\\"<br /><br />In \\"We Are Alive\\" bygone beings who are weary of the same old graveyard garb confess a longing to stop being alone in the dark every night with worms crawling in and out of them. Taking matters into their own bones, they end this record by putting on a dusty old 45 and rocking around the tombstones to the tune of spaghetti western music  seasoned with Johnny Cash. It is an interesting song. Listen closely and you'll hear Don Quijote, singing at the top of his voice, \\"Bring on Your Wrecking Ball.\\"

0439023521|4.0|As a newbie novelist, I have read several books on novel construction. And I also have a keen interest in reading low ratings of novels (one or two stars) to harness some insight on what didn't work for an author's particular book.<br /><br />After reading through all the one star reviews of The Hunger Games, there are two main points that I would like to address. I'm hoping my thoughts will give a better understanding of this novel's construction and why it was written in such a form. However, as stated in the title of my review, this does NOT pertain to those readers who are offended by the novel's main premise - children between the ages of 12 to 18 who are forced to kill each other - for which, as a parent, I can completely understand.<br /><br />Also, to make certain my hunch of this novel's writing was correct, I downloaded the first chapter of The Hunger Games. I read through most of the first chapter (and I found it quite compelling, pulling me right in to Katniss' life and description of her world), and plan on reading more.<br /><br />1. To all those one star reviewers who claim this novel has horrible writing (no great prose or \\"poorly written\\", lack of a use of sophisticated words, words and phrases repeated many times, etc.) - this story is written in the first person point of view, i.e. Katniss' point of view. Katniss is a teenage girl, of (I'm going to assume, since I haven't read the entire book yet) average to above average intelligence. And since it is from HER point of view, we, as readers, need to realize this. She will describe her world in her words, NOT Suzanne Collins words. What this means - The Hunger Games is not a novel which displays Suzanne Collins' artsy, advanced (or however you want to describe it) writing ability! Suzanne is telling this story through Katniss, and whatever writing ability, speaking ability, and general personality characteristics Katniss possesses - these will create the prose you read in this novel. And, similarly,this applies to Stephenie Meyer's Twilight series novels - Bella is speaking her mind, in her own words, and living her life in her own words (although, to be fair, I haven't read more than a few chapters of these books, so I don't know for certain if this was Stephenie's guiding template).<br /><br />Trust me, the moment an author intrudes upon a character, placing gracious, flowing prose, and large, advanced words, just to show \\"hey, look at me, am I not a super-intelligent and magnificent writer?\\", when the actual character would never use such words, neither in thoughts nor in speech, this is the moment a reader will feel a destructive distance and unease from the story and the character(s). We, as authors, need to keep it real!<br /><br />2. To all those one star reviewers who claim this novel was a rip-off of Battle Royale...oh boy, this is quite annoying. Do I believe Suzanne Collins copied parts of Battle Royale? Well, I can't say for certain, but, as a writer, I will tell you my feelings - the last thing I would ever want to do is copy another author's work! Blaahh!!! I would never want to write again if I had to do that! If an author cannot create a story from scratch, that is meaningful and somewhat precious to the author, in one means or another, then all may be lost. We all want to be original, don't we???<br /><br />BUT, that said, here is the catch-22. From several of my online readings on novel construction, one thing I learned very early is there are no truly original stories left anymore. Why? Simply because human beings' thoughts and dreams overlap. Yes, we all want to be original, but with so many billions of people upon this planet, the luck of the draw predicts your original story will most likely be similar to a story written before. And I truly believe, though I can't say with absolute certainty, this is the case of the similarities between The Hunger Games and Battle Royale. Suzanne wrote a story, from her own thoughts and from her own viewand interpretations of real-life situations. And, unfortunately (at least for many of the one star reviewers - some readers actually enjoy when stories are similar), the two stories are quite alike.<br /><br />So, one star reviewers, I hope this will enlighten, and maybe change, your disgruntled thoughts on The Hunger Games, only if, as I mentioned earlier, you are simply unhappy with the story because of \\"poor writing\\" or its similarity to Battle Royale. If you are unhappy with the story's main plot, young people fighting to the death with only one remaining, then you need to find stories that fit your comfort zone.

B0072GPQNG|5.0|Steven Spielberg is a grade above most contemporary directors because he keeps his priorities right, on the story and relationships. I hardly ever see movies in theaters today because most of them are crass and confusingly edited. This movie's cinematography (by Janusz Kaminski) is rich and creative, and close-ups are used effectively to help you bond with the characters. John Williams' music is once again enriching. It is a movie about the adventures of a horse in World War I, but it's even more about the struggle to adapt and maintain stable relationships in war. The horse is, for some of the characters, their closest companion. Kind of like \\"Sarah, Plain and Tall: Winter's End\\", War Horse's theme is about the need to build relationships as the fragility of life is impressed upon us. In fact, at one point, the horse brings together the opposing sides in friendship in the \\"no man's land\\" between the trenches, reminiscent of the widespread real-life Christmas Truce which occurred in 1914. Another thing that impresses me is the way it shows violence. There's no blood on the whole movie that I saw, and powerful emotions of fear and relief are created through making you feel attached to the characters, not through graphic violence. As a John Ford aficionado, I could see Ford's influence in Spielberg's storytelling, with the camera angle and position sometimes depicting an emotional closeness with the land and an ending told without words, like the end of Ford's movie The Searchers. It left me with a wonderful feeling at the end of satisfaction and peace. Way to go, Steven Spielberg!

0691142165|4.0|I've just finished reading This Time Is Different. I should say I `finished' reading about two thirds of the book as I skimmed or skipped the most technical portions. The book, as per its subtitle: Eight Centuries of Financial Folly, bills itself as comprehensive look at sixty six countries and their financial crises over almost all of the last millennia. Either due to its marketing or my presumptions, I thought the book would be a narrative of pithy coverage of financial crises. Rather, the book is quite technical with numerous charts, graphs, and economic analysis; the first 50 pages are devoted solely to setting up the financial rubric the book will use to analyze its data points. As such, I don't recommend the book for anyone looking for a financial history, or something like The Big Short or The Lords of Finance (as I was). If you're looking for a technical, yet mostly approachable from a lay perspective, analysis of financial crises and how they relate to today, it's great.<br /><br />Despite my misgivings, I do think the most valuable portion of the book (for me) was the last section which accumulated the information from past experience and applied it to the Great Recession. The book came out in 2009, so it doesn't have a great deal of information about how the late-2000s crisis turned out. Yet, the book does have a good deal of information about other major banking crises and how they ended. Obviously, this type of information is quite timely. And on this account, the current situation doesn't seem so bad, and there are signs for optimism. Major banking crises, apparently, typically raise unemployment by 7% and unemployment grows for 4.8 years, on average. The US unemployment rate (U1) rose from around 4% to 10%, about average, but unemployment has grown for only around two years. Much of this is probably due to systemic causes, but it does undercut almost all of the standard Republican-party economic voodoo, and the deranged `expansionary austerity' that has hurt much of Europe. There's a lot of analysis like this, and with a lot of the recent good economic news, it's interesting to see how that stacks up compared with past crises.<br /><br />The book's most valuable message conveys is broader than just a technical comparison of the present with the past, but is apparent from the book's title: the past does repeat itself, and did so in 2007. The Great Recession was unique in certain ways, but followed a very particular pattern and carried numerous warning signs. Yet, as with many historical crises, the people in positions of power, contrary to a lot of the demonizing rhetoric heaped upon banks, regulators, politicians, etc., seem to have disregarded this history, took up the kool-aid, and really believed that this time was different.  Yet, this time wasn't different, and it's folly to expect that in the future, that time will be anything but the same.

0312641893|5.0|\\"I'm not sure I would label it a 'survivor'\\" said Iko, her sensor darkening with disgust. \\"It looks more like a rotting pumpkin.\\" - pg 47<br /><br />This is one of my favorite books that I have read this year - even though it doesn't officially release until 2012, which luckily isn't too far away!<br /><br />Fairytales - everyone knows them - the beautiful damsel in distress, the heroic young champion who saves her. But Cinder, is so not your classic fairytale. I love how Meyer reinvents the story Cinderella with such wonderful twists! She takes all the main points that everyone knows and tweaks them into a fabulous new story - which for our day and age is a million times better. It is still a fairytale in my mind - but so much more. It's drama, it's sci-fi, it's paranormal!!<br /><br />Cinder isn't just a young girl enslaved by a hateful stepmother and her two daughters. She is also a cyborg. A member of a minority of people who have had parts of their body replaced with cyber engineered parts. She suffered an accident when she was very young and was saved by these advanced in medical technology. Unfortunately, this minority is treated rather harshly by the rest of the populace. This is a futuristic Earth, based on the year in the book, at least over a hundred years into the future. The world has warred, changed, Nations have been combined and some cease to exist. Technology has advanced such that we have hover cars, AI robots and a lot more. So with that we are given a sci-fi aspect to the story.<br /><br />Cinder lives in the Eastern Commonwealth which appears to be the bulk of all the former Asian countries combined. There are still politicians and nobility, doubt the world will ever get away from that - Plus we needed our prince now didn't we? So perhaps there will be a bit of romantic feelings developed? I'm not telling - but the interactions and acquaintance formed between Cinder and Prince Kai (Prince of the Eastern Commonwealth) - are interesting. Both Cinder and Prince Kai's characters were fleshed out so well that I didn't feel like there were any wasted pages what so ever. Often, I find that supporting lead characters are never done quite so well as the main protagonist. Not so here.<br /><br />The descriptions in this book are perfectly executed in my mind. Particularly, I enjoyed the description of the market. I could feel the masses of people going about their business, hawking their wares, rushing here and there. I still got that sense of a busy street market like we have today in Asian countries, but also filled with a lot of technology. Meyer really made it a point to add nuances of several different Asian cultures into the Eastern Commonwealth and I thought she blended them so nicely.<br /><br />Now to add some more drama to he mix - there happens to be a very nasty plague that has been afflicting the inhabitants of Earth for some number of years. Her adopted father was stricken with this illness right after she was taken in some 10 or so years ago. Others close to her, (who shall remain nameless - no spoilers allowed) are also afflicted. The incubation of the illness and subsequent death of the patient is very short - usually a matter of days. Currently, there is no vaccine or cure. I came away from most of my reading, despite the authors encouragement, feeling that this was for someone who had a lot of time to commit to this, was interested as a well-loved hobby (though she points out ways to decrease unpleasant chores and make things run smoothly on their own), and someone with a lot of money to invest (my ballpark after reading and having never owned a large aquarium was 5-10grand easy if you actually want it to work well), and someone who didn't care if the system paid for itself within a few years.<br /><br />Give any checklists, shopping lists, \\"questions to ask your supplier\\" lists, etc.<br />In traditional gardening books I find this info often and it is usually quite handy<br /><br />Give any step by step photos<br />MOst photos show elaborate set ups and about 1/3 show close ups of nature that could be from any gardening book<br /><br />Give step by step installation instructions:<br />I was imagining instructions like one might find in a cook book or repair manual with each step laid out for getting me started, but the steps in this book are more \\"rules of thumb\\" that help you navigate the decision making process. There is little example nor practical details shared that help me build something over a weekend to try out the idea. If the author were to assume that some readers philosophical answer would be \\"I want the cheapest easiest system so I can start a project and be rolling by the end of a long weekend\\" I think a step by step guide on how to govern that project would be extremely helpful.<br /><br />After reading, I found more info of the sort I was seeking from the internet. I can see that the book has some helpful info if you chose a media systemvs. the other types (which are explained somewhat in the book), and if you are willing to do the cost/supply/how to research yourself and use this book to help you organize your endeavor.<br />I don't find this book is helpful if you are trying to find a small simple way to get started and you want detailed step by step instructions (like one might find in a cookbook), some guidance on costs, yields, and a collection of specific examples in photos or text that help you envision how people on budgets/small spaces made this work for them.<br />So should you buy this book? B000000YG5|5.0|During the mid 1950's the jazz genre was entering into a very different place. Not too long ago America's most popular music,it lost some of that public gained in the transition from swing to be-bop. There was brilliant music made. But not everyone wanted to hear it. With all the creative energy in the music splitting off in different directions after WW2,jazz wasn't just jazz anymore. There were all these new and different styles of the music:swing,be-bop,Dixieland,modal. And as time passed many many more. So it seemed any new musician who came along would be required to do something extremely daring and extremely original to \\"stay fresh\\". Coming straight from the be-bop era and playing with Miles Davis's early bands Sonny Rollins didn't seem incredibly worried about any of this. As a person whose interest in playing seemed to be more significant sometimes than his ego Sonny didn't seem as concerned on this album about any of that. Just about good playing.<br /><br />  Perhaps it had something to do with Sonny's mild distrust of the recording studio belief that jazz came off more fully in live performance that one of the things that makes this album so interest is how much Sonny holds back from hogging major solo space. Also he's not a flamboyant improviser either. Of course this album is known by a lot of people by it's opener \\"St.Thomas\\",one of the most memorable melodies in jazz at the time with it's shuffling salsa rhythms and Sonny's clever invention of melody. \\"Strode Rode\\" is one of the few numbers on here where Sonny also solos a lot. On the final two numbers something very different happens. \\"Moritat\\" (more commonly known as \\"Mack The Knife\\") and at this time commonly associated with Louis Armstrong  has Rollins really only playing the melody while he pretty much lets Max Roach's flamboyant drum soloing and pianist Tommy Flanagan hold the floor the majority of the time. And why not? They are of course tremendous players. The same thing happens on the slower blues based numbers \\"You Don't Know What Love Is\\" and \\"Blue Seven\\".<br /><br />  So in the end does this album open up any major new artistic territory for jazz?No it really doesn't. What it does do is find a quartet of some of jazz's most talented players of the time in Rollins,Flanagan,Max Roach and Doug Watkins on bass just doing basically the sort of musical set they might do while in performance. So in a way this could be seen more as an in the studio concert rather than a strictly studio based session. Of course almost all jazz at this time was recording this way with no overdubbing. And even though this is by no means a live album,something about the way the sound was captured really put a lot of emphasis on the the way Sonny Rollins presented his music. Sometimes a musicians approach to their sound,as opposed to the WAY they play is the most significant aspect of their talents. And I would have to say that about Sonny Rollins. He plays wonderfully,melodically and crisply with a great sense of bounce and swing to his sound as well. And that to me might be why this album is so beloved by so many jazz fans from all over.r blues based numbers \\"You Don't Know What Love Is\\" and \\"Blue Seven\\".<br /><br />  So in the end does this album open up any major new artistic territory for jazz?No it really doesn't. What it does do is find a quartet of some of jazz's most talented players of the time in Rollins,Flanagan,Max Roach and Doug Watkins on bass just doing basically the sort of musical set they might do while in performance. So in a way this could be seen more as an in the studio concert rather than a strictly studio based session. Of course almost all jazz at this time was recording this way with no overdubbing. And even though this is by no means a live album,something about the way the sound was captured really put a lot of emphasis on the the way Sonny Rollins presented his music. Sometimes a musicians approach to their sound,as opposed to the WAY they play is the most significant aspect of their talents. And I would have to say that about Sonny Rollins. He plays wonderfully,melodically and crisply with a great sense of bounce and swing to his sound as well. And that to me might be why this album is so beloved by so many jazz fans from all over.

0439023513|2.0|On the one hand, the book is a pretty realistic portrayal of what war is like, and of the trauma suffered by someone who has been responsible for the death of hundreds if not thousands of people. For anyone who has ever been in the military or had family members in the military, this was probably a pretty powerful novel.<br /><br />However, need a book always be entirely realistic to be good? Certainly that does not hold true for video games; if one were to make a \\"realistic\\" war game, most of the time the player would be sitting in bunkers or in stakeouts. Nothing remotely enjoyable about that. Does Mockingjay really need to portray the hours and hours of characters sitting around doing nothing, waiting for news? I want to be entertained when I read a novel; having to read about a character pace a room for a whole chapter does nothing for me. I can understand that being necessary depending on the scene, and book, but I felt that in this case, the novel was robbed of too much action.<br /><br />Then there is the ending. The confusing, thrilling, and ultimately pathetic ending. One of my biggest issues is the death of Coin. Katniss' assasination of her is a very interesting twist in the story, and I quite liked it. But for a novel that strives to be so realistic, why does it fail to acknowledge just how destabilizing Coin's death would have been? How is that Katniss can kill the woman who led her people to freedom is allowed to get off on a mere \\"insanity\\" charge? If someone were to have murdered Toussaint L'Overture immediately following his conquest of Haiti, do you think people would have allowed for a lengthy trial and ultimate acquittal? It seems incredibly far-fetched to me.<br /><br />Then, there is Peeta, and the all-too-easy finale. So he suffers weeks and weeks of intense torture, and just magically improves and gets away with a few nightmares every now and then? I don't think Katniss should have ended up with him at all. Throughout the whole novel, Peeta was dedicated to protecting Katniss. He WANTED to die for her. His final act should have been to kill himself rather than let his madness consume him at some vital point in the story; that would have been a realistic, poignant ending to the story. But Collins wanted to give us a storybook ending.<br /><br />Last but not least; Katniss. Katniss who really doesn't change at all from the rest of the series, suddenly camera perfect and strong when she needs to be, weak whenever it suits the plot. And just as naive and hypocrital about death as always. The death of a civilian is intolerable, but squadrons of Peacekeepers having their heads ripped off is treated with no more respect than a rodent being run over by a car. The Peacekeepers are treated and described as being inhuman throughout the novel. Collins purposefully chooses to ignore the humanity of the soldiers, when they are just human as any of the civilians burned to death in District 12. They all have families too, sons, daughters, wives. They are no different than Katniss' dad yet they are slaughtered wholesale without a second thought. And yet Collins has the audacity to bemoan the civilian casualites of war.<br /><br />One of the things that irked me the most about the theme of war in Mockingjay is that Collins discredits everyone in the novel who made the rebellion possible. Were it not for the mercilessness of President Coin, and the discompassionate death traps of Gale and Beetee, they would not have won. As Gale said, they are playing by the same rule book as Snow; and it is for THAT REASON that they are able to defeat Snow. If Katniss had been allowed to lead the rebellion they would have all been squashed right from the beginning. And Coin is the one that helped 13 survive and make the revolution possible. Yet somehow she ends up the true villain.<br /><br />Collins makes a valid point in that war takes a heavy emotional toll. However, she still preaches a pathetic, hopeless idealism that revolution canbe achieved without innocent casualities, and to the desired effect. She tries to realistically portray war, but fails to grasp the notion that to win a war you HAVE to be willing to be as merciless as Gale. Unfortunately, Gale is used by Collins as a metaphor for war-mongerels, and is exiled into a vague, uninteresting ending. Collins makes no attempt to rationalize his behaviour, only calling it \\"rage\\" and \\"fire\\" and leaving it at that.<br /><br />Yet he cared for Katniss just as much as Peeta or anyone else. He endured months of watching her embrace Peeta and yet still did everything he could for her. Peeta tried to kill himself repeatedly for her, knowing full well that Katniss would probably live out the rest of her life miserable and insane. Is that really kindness on his part, or a selfish desire to go out a martyr? After reading the novels, Peeta comes off as more and more immature and selfish, constantly begging for death and wallowing in the hopeless idealism of defying the capitol. Gale is the one that truly helps Katniss. He saves over 900 lives from District 12, including Prim and Katniss' mother. He is constantly on the front lines, fighting for the revolution so all Katniss has to do is appear in the propos. He takes bullets and bits to the neck for her. Sure, Peeta saves her life in the Games, but even then, he still tries to kill himself every moment possible rather then do something useful, like actually protect Katniss.<br /><br />Ultimately, Mockingjay attempts to reach new heights in young adult literature, but fails to truly grasp its own themes and leaves its characters to die in pathetic write-offs.

0307454568|5.0|When last we saw Lisbeth she was buried alive with three bullets lodged in her body, one of which was in her brain.  Now she is recuperating in a hospital, but she has no electronic links with the outside world.  Her wounded father lies in a nearby bed, just a few steps from hers and he is planning to finish the job on her.  Her psychotic, feels-no-pain brother is still at large and he would like some payback.  The secret section within Sapo is plotting to isolate her in a mental institution (or worse), while Blomkvist seeks to save Lisbeth, tell her full story, bring the secret section to justice and balance his personal life.  His sister is defending Lisbeth, but she has little trial experience and is working to secure Lisbeth's trust.<br /><br />Bottom line: The Girl Who Kicked The Hornet's Nest begins with an extremely broad canvas and manages a large number of intertwined plots and subplots as it moves, inexorably to its end.  Larsson is having his way with us.  He knows that we want Lisbeth out and about.  He knows that we want to see justice done (make that bloody justice with a capital J).  He also knows that we know that we are very likely to be satisfied, but he is determined to make us wait.<br /><br />The third novel in the Millennium trilogy (or, as I would term it, the Salander Saga) does offer the gratification that we have been hungry for over the course of 1500+ pages of text.  At the same time it delays that gratification so as it render it more sweet, more exquisite, more perfect.<br /><br />Some have complained that there is too little of Lisbeth in the third novel.  However, the claustrophobic setting and situation present her with challenges that make her actions all the more satisfying.  This is a novel built on slowly-building rhythms that will end in a final crescendo, with Lisbeth getting her day in court and her final moments with her horrific brother.  The film version is the finest of the three, in my opinion, remembering that it is a courtroom drama and not, e.g. a locked-room (locked-island) mystery like the first installment.  I believe that I also prefer the third novel, though the first two have wonderful moments and the overall conception is richly imagined and fully realized.<br /><br />There is enough exposition in the last novel to make it a standalone, but all readers should read all three, in sequence.  This is very fine stuff, but it is a different kind of novel than its predecessors and each should be judged on its own terms.  I would warn my writing students not to try to do what Larsson has done here--in effect written 655 pages which build to the equivalent of about 30 pages or less.  Larsson pulls it off, I believe, but most could not.<br /><br />Highly recommended.urtroom drama and not, e.g. a locked-room (locked-island) mystery like the first installment. Recorded at the historic Beacon Theatre in New York during his current world tour, Tal Bergman is on Drums, Carmine Rojas plays Bass & Rick Melick is at the Keyboards with very special guest appearances by Beth Hart, John Hiatt & Paul Rodgers!  Kevin \\"Caveman\\" Shirley who also produced Dust Bowl is again credited for the astounding sound mix that must be heard to be believed, and indeed everyone involved in the production of this sensational DVD should take a bow!<br /><br />Joe Bonamassa continues to fascinate his audience with his gifted guitar and passionate vocals, and whilst extending his own repertoire, he also makes sure he pays tribute to all the people who inspired him along the way.  Like all great musicians Joe Bonamassa knows it's not just what you play that's important, but how you feel and deliver it that matters... and this guy certainly knows how to do just that!<br /><br />Disc 1 (Feature) starts with an interesting opening sequence showing Joe Bonamassa busking '72nd St. Subway Blues' at the same location in New York.  He then joins his band at the Beacon Theatre to open the show with his pulsating 'Slow Train' followed by 'Cradle Rock', 'When The Fire Hits The Sea', 'Midnight Blues', 'Dust Bowl' and 'The River'.  Next enter one of the most powerful female blues/rock singers to emerge since Janis Joplin...
